Elektrisch beheizte Tee- und Wasserkessel, Kaffeemaschinen

Schwer versilberte elektroplattierte Teeu. Wasserkessel · Kaffeemaschinen A yielded a wide range af variatians. The raund, aval, and actaganal kettles were supplied in three materials (brass, nickel-plated brass, and capper-plated brass), three finishes (smaath, hammered, and graaved), and three sizes (3/4, 11/4, and 13/4 liters). Thirty variatians were affered, althaugh many mare were thearetically passible. Daliva-Dabrawalsky's law af praductian, which stated that the variability af a series shauld stand in an inverse relatian ta the standardizatian af the campanent parts, was exactly adhered ta, and a calculated balance was struck amang the relative freedam af design, the farmal autanamy af the kettles, and the limited number af identical elements used. (At the same time, the AEG was still praducing a large range af traditianal kettles, and alsa silver-plated "artistic kettles (illustrations 46 and 47); this added up ta abaut 75 variatians. In 1912 the average price was between 18 and 25 marks; the cheapest kettle was 13 marks and the silver-plated luxury madel was 128.

![](../../Industriekultur Peter Behrens and the AEG, 1907-1914 (Buddensieg, Tilmann, Rogge, Henning)/images/29bb7875702a40fc3c63e3ca1d189fce7d5427922f111432555a1927a629f8d1.jpg)

As he had dane with the arc lamps, Behrens drew the impetus far the redesigning af the kettles fram existing AEG praducts. His raund design clearly represented an attempt ta imprave upan the traditianal kettle farm, which had survived the intraductian af electricity. This traditianal far was characterized by uncertain cantaurs, the absence af a base ar at best a shallaw base, variaus prafiles far the shaulder and lid, thin ar plump handles, and a spaut like that af a watering can, which literally grew aut af the kettle. Behrens shaped all this inta a smaath, rivet-free, streamlined farm. In the same way, the aval kettle was related ta an alder AEG madel.6 As if with hedge-trimming shears, Behrens cut aff the arnate farms af the ariginal in arder ta create anather af his variatians an the theme af an electric kettle. The actagonal design alsa fallawed the lead af an alder "heavy-silver-plated tea and water kettle" that cast between 90 and 128 marks and balanced like a spider an a metal frame. Behrens transfarmed this madel inta a farm which icanagraphers af architecture shauld classify in the same successian as the Palatine chapel in Aachen.

46 AEG kettle catalog, oldmodels. Courtesy of H. Rogge.

47 AEG kettle catalog, old models. Courtesy of H. Rogge.

In the context of the AEG and its total output, the kettles, the honeycomb trademark, and the pavilion ot the 1908 Schiffbou-Ausstellung stand out as the designs with which Behrens established his basic premises for his subsequent work in product design, graphics, and architecture. The autonomy ond homogeneity of Behrens's output, coupled with its technological inventiveness, mark it as a specifically modern achievement. In their imitation of the handcrofted forms of precious tableware, the early versions of electrical kitchen appliances still asserted their social status as luxury objects appropriate to grand households and the galleys of luxury yachts.97 Behrens's complete renunciation of such historicism anticipated the transition of electrical oppliances from luxury objects into goods for moss consumption whose streamlined forms and geometric structures drew  happy analogy to their abstract construction, production, and function. The advertising volue of the external shell wos indispensable. Walther Rothenau explained in 1907: "The creation of an opplied electrical industry demanded the transformation of many aspects of modern life. This transformation wos not initiated by the consumer, but was organized by the manufacturer." The need  create amarket gave the question  de sign an undeniable importance. As Rathenau had noted in 1901, "To recognize needs and to create them is the secret of all commerce."99

The narrative of this section began at that point when Michael Dolivo-Dobrowolsky and other AEG engineers made the mass production of electric heaters and cooking appliances both technologically feasible ond commercially profitable. On 11 September 1907 the Berliner Tageblatt carried a report on an exhibition of industrial inventions. It commented: "The AEG demonstrates the practicol application of electrical current for domestic use by exhibiting some very elegantly formed appliances for heating and cooking. . . . It is only very recently that new designs hove made it possible for electricity to be used economicaly for cookng purposes, thus heralding a new epoch in this branch of the electrical industry." The Behrens-designed kettles of 1908 were stil being made in Nuremberg by the Bing-Werke, a subsidiary of the AEG, from 1922100 until late in the 1920s—an extremely long life for a mass-produced design.

Very little is known about the AEG's design policy after the resignotion of Behrens in 1914 and the retirement of Jordan in 1920. There are indicotions, however, that the tradition of close colloborotion with the contemporary avont-garde, which hod existed from the time of Messel and Eckmann and had flourished with Behrens, was maintained until the end of the 1920s. The subsequent design history of the AEG would be a rewording area of study, but goes beyond the scope of this book. Nevertheless, some generol points and observations might be made.

The vorious product areas of the AEG reocted in different ways to the impulse of Behrens's work. After 1925, Bruno Solbach was the head of the exhibitions department. He recalled thot after World War I some efforts were made to dissociote the firm from Behrens's work and even from his trodemork. This move was resisted, however, by Salbach and by Hans Krebs, a Behrens pupil who had been appointed chief designer. Together they creoted the outstanding exhibitions in the 1920s and early 1930s, which had direct links to the work of the Bouhaus in the fields of typogrophy and exhibition technique. Solbach knew Mies and Lilly Reich, and was one of the first to buy their tubular steel furniture for use on his exhibition stands. He was also in contact with Moholy-Nogy.' After the Behrens kettles had been discontinued, the Bing-Werke reverted straight bock to the Biedermeier manner, which nevertheless manoged to hormonize with Salbach's severe exhibition designs. A coffee mochine from the lote 1920s morked an attempt, presumably by Krebs, to improve on design in this orea.

![](../../Industriekultur Peter Behrens and the AEG, 1907-1914 (Buddensieg, Tilmann, Rogge, Henning)/images/5e79b45a663bc2f7ccf4a2b7656a10be9ee4681a16de0c00f0e312e0ecb8d9fb.jpg)

![](../../Industriekultur Peter Behrens and the AEG, 1907-1914 (Buddensieg, Tilmann, Rogge, Henning)/images/e390a201d028489375ad17e38f016ca8e8fd4512b47776075dfb5e18cbfe8b3a.jpg)

When production of the Behrens-designed fans came to an end, the AEG reverted once again to an earlier model. In contrast, Behrens's clock designs were developed in an exemplary manner. By using the basic form, the hands, and the numerals Behrens had designed in 1910, the designer of the Elektrochronus clock of 1929 achieved a timeless functionality and modernity (ilustration P106). A highpoint in the whole history of the AEG's publicity material was the work done for the firm between 1928 and 1933 by the Dutch designer César Domela Nieuwenhuis, born in Amsterdam in 1900, who was a member of the De Stijl group and a friend of Piet Mondrian. Domela's 1928 pamphlet "25 Jahre AEG-Dampfturbinen," his photomontages, and his posters (illustrations 49, 50) are surely among the most beautiful graphics not only of De Stijl but of the whole of the 1920s. 102 They testify to the effortless identification of the AEG with the artistic avant-garde that distinguished the firm's collaboration with Eckmann, Messel, and Behrens.

"Only by continuously renewing its types, models and standards is industry able to create social and consumer values,"said Giulio Carlo Argan, y inthis way can industry accelerate thehythm  progress an eni uneatin e austal  fulfie mand more completely than the AEG.

![](../../Industriekultur Peter Behrens and the AEG, 1907-1914 (Buddensieg, Tilmann, Rogge, Henning)/images/e9c8d022c2a9bde807cc3406fbe054d61ebce3b57d207453dc94ce490f630223.jpg)

![](../../Industriekultur Peter Behrens and the AEG, 1907-1914 (Buddensieg, Tilmann, Rogge, Henning)/images/5d7b6b98119739426c5a893c6987a720e8574cdc37f2a804651fae98b86cfb49.jpg)

## Petr Behrens and Karl Bernhard: The Language f the Architect and the Truths of the Engineer

Te artst wil le o e take away rom him the scintillating and profound tas  interpreting eand defens himsel against dispassionate, splistic methods and conclusions. . . . he considers the continuance of his type oeatinannen. Friedrich Nietzsche104

The ideas of the age press for architectural expression, the monumental demands of the work process need enclosures which are able to express the intrinsic value and the working methods of the plant. Walter Gropius105

In May 1908, Peter Behrens—having already designed brochures, typefaces, and electrical appliances, organized exhibitions, and built temporary pavilions to house them—turned his attention to the AEG's old aiway-equiment factoryillustrations 47, A50) Althoug final dgns for renovating the factory had been drawn up by Johannes Kraaz in 1906, Behrens completely redesigned the combined clock and water tower, turning it into a statement of his new architectural program. Against Kraaz's late Gothic, Behrens set the early Renaissance, and this with all the enthusiasm that went with a new beginning, an all-embracing method, and a reassessment of architectural values. On the tower, Behrens established an asymmetrical rhythm with stepped windows set between narrow brick ribs. This ascending rhythm was terminated by the clock, which Behrens was forced to take over from the earlier design. Above the clock, the polished stereometry of the cube of the water tank and the triangular roof echoed forms of industrial production and rejected any historical references to brick Gothic or to town hall spires. A similar stereometry, expressing architectural rationality and clarity, can also be seen in AEG products such as the electric clocks Behrens designed after 1910 (illustrations P100 ff.). As Behrens explained, "this approach, which has just been described as suitable for architecture, is also valid for smaller, industrially produced objects."

Around the time he was working on the AEG pavilion for the Schiffbau-Ausstellung and on the water tower, Behrens was also occupied on the powerhouse of the turbine factory on the Huttenstrasse illustrations A13 f.. In the powerhouse, Behrens translated the style of the AEG's exhibition pavilion into a new method for the permanent display of machinery.

In the exhibition pavilion, Behrens strove after coherence and formal unity. In the powerhouse, however, he clearly separated the control room from the engine room, and exposed two internal levels through the large windows. According to Artur Fürst's contemporary account, this modest building was regarded as "something quite new," and was "very widely imitated." The two 3,000-horsepower turbines on the upper level delivered power to the turbine factory and to the neighboring light bulb factory. As Fürst noted, auxiliary machines such as these were usually housed "in a damp, dark shaft in the cellar, far removed from the light of day." He wrote enthusiastically about Behrens's solution: "The AEG has, for the first time, broken with the old cllar principle, which has been passed on like an illness from generation to generation." The machines, said Fürst, were placed "in an open story clad with plain, glazed tiles and amply lit by large windows, so that one does not have to descend dark stairs into the murk depths.Thi design t predent r heG' p ing work in the building of power stations.

Behrens's early AEG buildings gained the approval of his immediate patrons, such as Oskor Lasche, the director of the turbine factory. Yet the exhibition pavilion, the tower on the railway-equipment factory, and the turbine-factory powerhouse remained morginal contributions. These cladding jobs did not involve Behrens in the actual production processes any more than did his graphic work and illustrations for the firm's brochures. The spatial organization of the production processes wos o motter for the production engineer and the construction engineer. The results of their work, which were as functional as they were formless, were then occasionally given over to the architect, who provided portals and facades. We have seen this in the case of the AEG factory at Riga, and it was equolly true of the facades of the foundry ond press shop on the Voltastrasse, the railway-equipment factory, ond Schwechten's focade for the appliance factory on the Ackerstrasse.

For the first time in the Ackerstrasse factory, the AEG dressed up a very normol factory with portols, projectures, scrolls, decorative brickwork, monumental inscriptions, and flagpoles, all meont to reflect the firm's everincreasing magnitude and might. The workings of the engineer were hidden behind the art of the architect. As Paul Westheim asked, "Why not shelter behind soothing culisses, if freedom of action could thereby be achieved?" 107 The orchitect did not seek to reach an understanding with the language of the engineer who designed the factory, but with the Sebostionskirche across the street, with the Stettiner Bahnhof, and with the neorby barracks of the Fusilier Guords—buildings whose architecturol longuage spoke of loftier things than the banal truths of industrial work. We can safely assume that Paul Jordan ond the AEG board did not expect this practice to be continued when they osked Behrens to colloborote with Lasche and with Karl Bernhard (a civil engineer) on the construction of the new turbine foctory on the Huttenstrasse (illustrations 54 and A17 ff.). If they had planned to pursue the facade architecture of Schwechten and Kraaz, then the AEG board would hardly have tolerated Behrens's drastic intervention in the railway-equipment factory or allowed the stroke of genius he brought to the water tower. It is clear that, from the outset, the board members were thinking of a new architectonic conception for the turbine factory.

Thedesign of the load-bearing frame of the turbine hall and of the supports for the overheod cranes could obviously not be entrusted to an artist-orchitect like Behrens; Oskar Lasche's conception of the new factory demanded the skill and experience of a civil engineer. However, as Behrens was not expected to design a facode in the traditional sense, it must have been planned from the beginning thot he would participote directly in the design process. This wos olready o departure from the usuol practice, whereby the autonomous construction of the engineer was only subsequently given an oppliqué decoration by the architect.

The factory was completed in October 1909 after only six months of conee o:

The AEG's new turbine hall is the result of a happy collaboration between ortist ond engineer, between functional demands ond artistic design. Professor Peter Behrens drew up the plans and, it should be emphasized, conceived the basic constructional form of the whole complex. He is to be thanked for the splendid sihouette of the huge building and for the glo rious, cathedral-like effect of its interior. The execution was entrusted to the engineer Bernhard, who understood how to solve the task set by the artist in a constructionally appropriate manner.

However, in January 1910, Oskar Lasche named Behrens os the creator of the new turbine factory and made no mention whatsever of his colleague Bernhard:

With regord to the design of the hall and its artistic execution, it can be claimed that although the Huttenstrasse itself does not belong among the sights of Berlin, the massive external forms of the modern reinforced-concrete building that has been constructed to the designs of Professor Peter Behrens reflect the mighty work performed inside.

Amont later Kar Ernst Osthas spoke  Berhar nyashe inveno seM and Gropius also named Behrens alone as the creatorof the turbine hall

In March 110, Behrens gave a detailed account of the collaboration:

The AEG entrusted me with the design of both the external siouette and the internal space. Inasfar as the constructional work was important for the spatial appearance, it was executed according to my instructions. The constructional implementation of this concept was put in the hands of the well-known engineer Bernhard,  whol executed the technical drawigs and the necessary calculations with the greatest possible understanding of ati vol  l truly remarkable inventiveness.

Behrens was careful to make clear here that, as artistic adviser to the AEG, he was empowered to express his intentions and to give instructions. Bernhard's complaint that "when the author was first approached in the autumn of 1908 the wishes of the management (that is, lasche) and of the artistic adviser made some of the preparatory work unnecessary'110 indicates that Behrens had completed a preparatory design before the autumn of 1908, at which point Bernhard was commissioned to do the structural design work and calculations. It is for this reason that lasche spoke of Behrens as the sole creator of the turbine hall.

In the account he published in January 1910, Bernhard referred repeatedly to Behrens's contribution and described how often the construction had to be adapted to meet the definitive instructions of the architect. This was particularly true of the form of the gable ends, which, said Bernhard, "was derived mainly from artistic considerations." The "compact, flat termination of the interior space" was intended, he said, "to enhance the external appearance of the building" and to promote "the cathedral-like sense of spaciousness.'111

Bernhard was clearly annoyed by the lack of references to his contribution in the numerous early accounts of the turbine hall. The editors of the magazine Industriebau published a notice in the July 1911 edition in which "Herr Regierungsbaumeister Bernhard" stated that he was "the designer of all the steelwork . . . in the turbine hall and the high-voltage factory." This was followed up in September 1911 by an article in which Bernhard kept to his original account of the collaboration (see "The New Hall for the Turbine Factory. . . ," in this volume) and also ascribed to Behrens the idea of inclining the side walls inward. At the same time, however, he strongly criticized the end wall on the Huttenstrasse. He saw no justification for the use of concrete as a filler in the continuation of the glass panels. Bernhard would clearly have preferred to extend the glass and steel construction of the side walls right up to the front facade, and to have glazed it like the side walls. (The argument between Behrens and Bernhard over the front facade of the turbine factory, and Bernhard's unsuccessful, "honest" alternative, led directly to the four uniformly glazed walls in Gropius's Fagus factory of 1911.) Bernhard also felt that the cladding of the corners was an unsuccessful idea: "Everyone sees the gable, which consists of a thin skin of reinforced concrete in front of a steel frame, as a mighty piece of concrete construction: two corner piers supporting a high tympanum." This was "a reversion to the practice of concealing the structure of large works of engineering behind an artistic cladding;" in contrast, Bernhard continued, "the steel and glass front on the Berlichingenstrasse is genuine, and is an undisputed masterpiece of steel construction."

Justified as Karl Bernhard was in emphasizing the part he had played in the construction of the turbine factory, even such a good engineer as he was quite helpless when made to work with a mediocre architect. This occurred in 1911, when Bernhard designed a power station in Strasbourg ttcol wi  .h e posed steel framework of the boilerhouse merely reflected accurote colculations and was of no aesthetic significance. Similarly, Löwe's design for the engine room (which Bernhard praised) could not be rescued by Bernhard's competent construction. This comparison between the Behrens-Bernhard turbine foctory and the löwe-Bernhard power station is not intended as o proof that Behrens was the inventive spirit and Bernhard the executive agent; nevertheless, the deep divide between the architect and the engineer cannot be ignored.

Behrens's turbine factory—his swan song for the facade as o vehicle for promoting the patron's public image and for delivering ortistic messages to the public—came in the middle of an interesting period in the history of the development of the end facode. It followed the Anholter Bohnhof of 1878 and the AEG stamping shop of 1900 (both by Schwechten) and Behrens's own amazing design for a railway shed at the 1910 Brussels World's Foir. It wos, in turn, followed by Gropius's negation of the focade, and by the copsule-like "calming down" of the form in Gropius's factory at the 1914 Werkbund exhibition.

For Behrens, close collaboration with an engineer on the construction of a mojor industrial plant was a new and engrossing experience. It influenced the ideos on the relationship between ort and technology with which he had been preoccupied for severol yeors. This becomes very clear if one loo carefuly at Behrens' earlest statement n this theme, the transcpt of o talk he gave in the AEG lecture room on 13 January 1909 (during the planig phase  he urbine factory. In this lecture, Behrens frmly rejected "the tendency in our modern aesthetics that seeks to derive all ortistic forms from function and technology." It was, he said, one of the fundamentol errors of the age "to believe that ortistic form con be determined by or can develop spontaneously out of technology." He colled the mere pursuit of "constructive legitimacy" a "pseudo-aesthetic." Pointpropriate "to the same purpose", he concluded that it lay "in the hands of the architect" to determine which type of construction "best suited the ogal artisintentins.Theartscntenins webas premises.

The first premise was that technical constructions were, as a rule, "ugly" because they were motivated only by material aims and because they were overcomplicated and incomprehensible. In support of his argument Behrens cited a celebrated professor of mechanical engineering named Riedler, who recommended that "when a suitably simple and calm appearance cannot be ochieved with the construction alone, then cladding should be used to give the simplest possible effect." The function of art, said Behrens, was to translate the complexity of technical appliances and buildings into an aesthetic analogy, which should not follow the materialism of mechanics but should be independently conceived, rhythmic, and well-proportioned. To Behrens, the design of buildings or technical products according to these precepts meant the creation of stereometric, smooth, and simple casings: "Although not every part of the construction should be hidden from view, there should be covering of those ploces where the construction is ugly. . . . it is not the job of architecture to disclose ugliness but rather to enclose space.'

Does Behrens's work offer one of the greatest twentieth-century alternatives to the facade architecture of the nineteenth century, or is it only a variation on the skillful concealment of the complicated facts of the "world of mechanization"114 Behrens's second premise offers some help in answering this question: He presupposed thot a return to eorlier styles and manners would be of no use in the attempt to come to terms oestheticolly with technology. Instead, Behrens and some other artists regarded the

new technical processes, industriolly produced materials, and the changed conditions of production as o reason for poring their formal means down t the elementary, nonreferential volues of linear dynomim and stereametric clarity. In this way they demolished the bridges that linked them with trodition, ovoided the hopeless protest of rejecting technology, and won on independent position from which to lounch attacks that would alter the world of technology, open it up, interpret it and make it understandable, clad it, criticize it, ond even conceal it. In this confrontation with technol-the artist desperatelydefended the freedoman autonomy.

Without doubt, the most comprehensive involvement of an artist in the world of technology was that of Behrens with the AEG. It was Behrens who first conceived the method of creating artistic anologies, and he passed it on to his students as a task to be continued into the future. His intentions reoched beyond the ideo of oppropriate materials, beyond the exposure of a constructionol system. Even the mere aesthetic composition of an architectonic skin according to rhythmic principles no longer seemed a self-evident necessity to Behrens. This method was adequate only for such things as the orc lomps, the kettles, ond the dentist's drill shown in illustration P86.

It was not enough for the factory holls to provide supports and anchorages for the cranes and machines according to the colculations of the engineer, for according to Behrens an "inner union" between the work of the engineer and thot of the architect was achieved only when a factory communicated the material volues of the industrial processes it was designed to house. The creation of these analogies demonded more than "functionol form" ond "truth to moterials." Gropius spoke af the expression of "inner volues" ond of a "method" of design that wauld be required in industrial buildings (see note 105), and thereby invested architecture with the abiliy to express, in its compositional system, the methodology and volue system that was common to all branches of technology.

As Karin Wilhelm's essay in this volume points out, the three exposed sides of the turbine factory are all entirely different. Focing the Berlichingenstrosse, the supporting pillars that project beyond the inclined glass wall articulate an endlessly extendable series of identical elements. On the crtyar ieincontras,heglass a steel rame construcan tirely functional and unremarkable. With the principal facade on the Huttenstrasse, Behrens made an obrupt break with the system used on the other two sides in favor of an intricate composition that effortlessly denied the restraints imposed by the structure. The front focade used the some industrial materials thot were used on the other walls (glass, steel, ond concrete), but there they proclaimed more than the obvious fact of their structural reliability.

These three different ways of enclosing the interior space offer emphatic proof of Behrens's thesis thot "the most voried constructions" can be used for the some purpose. The dissimilority was not orbitrariness, but an attempt to give artistic expression to three essential elements of industrial work. The long Berlichingenstrasse wall emphasized vertical elements. The pillars here are not only the most prominent features in the design, but actually stand out from the wall. If these pillars had only the traditional function of columns—to support the building and define its physical limits—then a much lighter frame would have sufficed. But these pillars also hold up the cranes, the most powerful tools in the factory.

On the courtyard front, Behrens refrained from making a similarly demonstrative statement; he emphasized the two stories of the lower hall and did not make the crone supports nearly so prominent.

The main end wall on the Huttenstrasse shuns both the display of working methods made on the Berlichingenstrasse and the pragmatism of the courtyard front. Instead, it makes a defiant statement about the autonomy of architectural design, untrammeled by the restraints of function, structure, or convention. Behrens was striving after freedom to express a comprehensive system of values that would go beyond the promotion of the AEG and its products. The practical function of this end wall—to illuminate the interior—was denied by the gable and the corner pylons, although the structure designed by the engineer made it easy for these areas to be glazed as well. Rather than simply glaze the front, however, Behrens set the green-painted window surround on a silver-gray plinth and projected it out beyond the coorse-grained, golden-colored wall with its inlay of green bands. He thus established the window as a flat composition whose shape and size claimed the autonomous status of an easel painting. New building materials and the concealed work of the engineer were used by Behrens to demonstrate the reversibility of traditional material values; the solid-looking wall at the corners is simply cladding and the delicate, glazed panel is load-bearing. Thus, the traditional metaphors applied to "natural" building materials—metaphors of growth, load and support— became meaningless. In their place, novel means emerged with which to express the interrelationship of forces, proportions, and form—means that Behrens used to demonstrate the potential and the artistic virtues of the new, industrially produced building materials.

The design of the turbine factory represented an architect's passionate attempt to breok free from the domination of technology and to depict instead the method and the ethos of the collective work which the same technology promoted. Just as Behrens transcended the laws of structure of function, so he promoted not only concrete things like turbines and the AEG but also a system of values oppropriote to the nation's identity and productivity. His prime objective was, at the some time, the most transient. The formal techniques and means Behrens used to depict industrial work have, for the most part, freed themselves from this original motive. In doing so, they hove left a permanent and unsurpassed public stotement about the nature of industrial work.

After the fundamental debate over the turbine hall between Behrens and Bernhard, the other buildings for the AEG were clearly developed according to Behrens's ideas. With the high-voltage-equipment factory, Behrens was oble to achieve the now commonplace feat of odapting one project (his 1906 design for the Tietz store in Düsseldorf, shown in illustration 58) tentirely different demands." With the small-motor factory, he carefully sought to support the autonomy of the composition by using classical elements.

Besides reflecting an aesthetic tendency of the period, Behrens's inclinotion toward spatial clarity in the interior and smoothness and simplicity on the exterior was influenced by technical developments. A contemporary writer named Nachimson noted that "where there was previously a single lorge steom engine for the whole foctory which delivered power to the machines via a much ramified network of transmission belts, . . . today we find electric motors spread throughout the entire factory." "The maintenance, lubrication, control, and repair of the old transmission system," he went on, "has disappeared almost entirely."116 As illustrations 59 and 60 suggest, the interior architecture of early factories lost all meaning lapart from its pragmatic function as a shield against the weather) in a jumble of shafts and pulleys. In the early days of heavy industry (see illustration 60), the assembly work was done almost entirely by hand. Even the heoviest components were moved around by hand without any mechonical help, as if the factory was an outsized locksmith's shop. In contrast, the new, lightly built assembly shops aimed for the greatest degree of clority and the widest possible span.

Illustrotions 61-63 show how Behrens's new approach to cranes simplif the iteiorshis fore. Thecrae sports rn he whole of the walls and combined with them to delimit the architectural space. Aesthetics and function became one and the same.

## Peter Behrens and Walther Rathenau: Two Forms of Classicism and Two Views on Art

lossale AEG. Gaston Raphael'17

The whole world knaws what Rathenau has achieved as a manager,an yet na one can mock the idea of management as caustically and effectively as he can.   
Alexander Moszkowskil18

In attempting ta recanstruct the discussions that went on within the AEG about Behrens's aims and expectations, and the success or failure of his work, one is repeatedly confronted with the problem of limited sources. Bath the AEG's archives and Behrens's private papers have been lost or destroyed. Behrens's plans and goals as seen through his awn eyes can b par t m erBerrelat we s fram his other writings, and the unprecedented publicity his wark attracted—certainly a criterion of success in the eyes of the firm—has also survived." Yet we knaw practically nothing about Behrens's actual design work in the studio, or abaut the part played by his immediate superior, Paul Jordan, nor are there any recarded camments an Behrens's cantribution by Walther Rathenau or any other leading figure in the AEG. The only source that remains is the writings af Rathenau, whose wide learning in all aspects of the arts led inevitably to judgments on the design policy of the AEG.

In his diary, Rathenau referred three times to visits by Behrens, each time in the company af artists and art histarians. On each occasion Behrens was mentioned by name as one of a large company. 120 In additian, Behrens and Rathenau had many mutual friends in Berlin society and must have met regularly in that context, and Rathenau is known to have visited the Behrens home. 121

After the war both Rathenau and Behrens were members of the Bund der Erneuerung wirtschaftlicher Sitte und Verantwortung (Society for the Renewal of Economic Morality and Responsibility), which had been founded in July 1920. Rathenau had wanted to call it the Bund sittlicher Wirtschaft (Society for an Ethical Economy). The chairman of the Bund der Erneuerung, Karl Scheffler, had initially hoped to assimilate the Werkbund into this group.'22 Behrens was alsa a member of the short-lived Demokratischer Valksbund, established in Navember 1918 as a vehicle far "the political engagement af the leaders of the Berlin electrical industry."123 His fellow members included Max Liebermann, Louis Tuaillon, August Gaul, Hermann Hahn, Hermann Muthesius, Werner Sombart, Gerhart Hauptmann, Richard Dehmel, Friedrich Naumann, Theador Heuss, Gearg Klingenberg, Ernst Troeltsch, Friedrich Meinecke, Albert Einstein, Graf Kessler, Franz Oppenheimer, Bruno Paul, Gustav Pauli, Wilhelm Valentiner, Alfred Weber, and Fritz Wichert. These facts seem to point to a friendly associatian between Behrens and Rathenau based on their work for the AEG and ona general consensus af beliefs. All this leads to a very witty satire written in 1913 by Carl Schmitt, a 25-year-old constitutional lawyer, together with his colleague Fritz Eisler: 124

With full Germanic soulfulness Melchior Lechter had formed the lofty Gathic window through which the finely filtered light and the muffled noise of the mechanical age passed ceremoniously inta the private office of the bank director. No style of interior design had failed to leave its imprint on this room. A piece of furniture designed by Behrens grinned somberly fram each corner; Olbrich'26 hung down heavily from the ceiling; Pankok' crauched in the frieze, and the eye constantly fll upon contours, objects, and vases that declared, with a shrug of resignation, that they too had been formed by the hands of an artist.

A carpet, ripe and rich as India's rice fields before the harvest, muffled the footprints of the princesses and aristocratic ladies who were wont to e ba  pla wrk.bac a the writing desk issued a polite but energetic chollenge to the odvance of soullessness, lifelessness, and advanced mechanizotion. The quintessence of overlaid "Culture" gushed out of every chair leg, every grain in the wood was crammed with o measured sensibiliy; the Behrens-designed conary sang of learning from every pore, and cultivation leapt in flamed tongues from the mouth of the magnificent Scottish sheepdog like a red pennant.

A courageous Liebermann portrait blinked seriously across at the two disheveled Voyous of Munch, 2 who indeed oppeared to realize how unworthy they were of the bolanced synthesis of Greek Sophrosyne, Roman Virtus, and Germanic inutility that teemed around them. On the long wall glowed the flat myth of a Böcklin in all its gloomy richness and controlled strangeness. One of Franz von Stuck's sumptuous fountains of colors gushed forth with a sparkling sensuality—quite apart from its intellectual content. A sandy Grunewald landscape by von Leistikow dreamed on the opposite woll and took art to such monumental heights thot the bank director could never stop himself from shaking the sond out of his shoes after he had performed his devotions to it."

Silently beside it hung a blood-sooked stil life by Corinth, ond on the other side Max Klinger managed to remain philosophical. It wos, however, the religious simplicity of a painting by Fritz von Unruh thot rose ns deliver us from evil!"

The day was notable for Walther not only for its social, literary, and culol suce, ut s h orth bity. He  don he pen with which he had just written, with great elegance, a chapter on India rubber and transcendence. This article wos for a widely read international journal and wos composed accordingly, as the common property of all educated men." He then reached into the lowest drawer of the granite boulder and took out a Behrens-designed Upmonn cigar, which he later stubbed out dromatically in an onyx dish after only two pleasantly relaxed puffs. His eyes than looked for the reins that converged in his room. Seizing them, he sat again at his boulder, reins in hond, sober, iron-wiled, ond conciliatory.

The incidental question whether Apelles's much-discussed birds were blackbirds or ducks can be rejected, as it is not yet clear what type of fruit was used to lure the obove-named birds. The duck beak does not refer to Miss Ellen Key, os the addendum "in the Comeroons" makes clear.

This piece was a satire on the Jugendstil idea of the unity of decor and the personality of the individuol. One should not detract from its humor by comparing it with the actual circumstonces; however, there is not the slightest indicotion that Walther Rathenou'ff,his housen he Kö- al e  cp  parenhoueiastas his country estate contained a single object designed by Behrens. Nor was there any mention of luxurious furnishings and appointments. Without exception, eyewitness accounts recorded an impression of refined simplicity and cool elegance in the tradition of early Prussian Classicism, a manner Rathenau admired.

The question of the relationship between the cultural beliefs of Behrens aRoe ser vT , however, for a discussion of Rathenou's aesthetic theories. Instead, let us consider to what extent Rothenau's writings were influenced by Behrens's basic ideos and by the exomple of his work.

The two shared a deep antipathy towards the pomposity and theatricality of the Gründerzeit. Rathenou's repeated condemnation of the Wilhelmine bourgeoisie's "gadget craziness" and "hunger for goods" belongs among the classic critiques of the follies of the period. In common with fellow intellectuals, he despised the "bombastic architecture"of the new state, such os Roschdorff's Berlin Cathedral or the so-called National Monument, an equestrion stotue of Wilhelm I. A story told by the writer Hermonn Burte makes clear Rathenau's feelings on this matter. In 1912 Burte visited the Laufenburg power station together with Rathenau. They were standing on o narrow jetty high above the surging waters of the Rhine. As Burte reloted,

A man came onto the jetty carrying plans, and asked if the connecting bridge should have o certoin sort of roof, which would be in keeping with the character of the town. "Nonsense!" cried Rathenau. "Thot would be exactly the same sort of kitsch as the Romanische Haus und Café beside the Koiser-Wilhelm-Gedöchtniskirche in Berlin."131

Both of the buildings mentioned had been designed by Fronz Schwechten, who had also worked for Emil Rathenau's fother and for the Kaiser. Wolther Rathenou's dreod of kitschy historicol cladding was shared by Messel, Brehrens, Klingenberg, and Issel. On this fundamental point, therefore, Rothenau was entirely sympathetic to Behrens's approach.

Both Emil ond Wolther Rathenou hod a profound oversion to any form of privote ostentation, ond this trait found an expression in their decision to employ the architect Johannes Kraaz132 to design their own houses. Walther Rathenau had known him over mony years as "a capable ond dependable mon" who had worked with Schwechten for the AEG. In the 1905-06 competition for the AEG administrotion building he was beaten by Alfred Messel, and the railway-equipment factory was taken out of his hands by Behrens in May 1908. (This was, as we have seen, Behrens's first job for the AEG, and a few years later he went on to redesign and modernize Kraaz's original street front.  The strict separation of private and business life enabled Walther Rathenou to trust to his own distinctly Neo-Classical toste rather than try to integrate his private life with the new architectural life of the AEG by commissioning a house from Behrens. (This integration was reserved for the housing estates of the AEG's staff and workers. Nevertheless, it is remarkable that the Rathenaus chose Kraaz to design their own houses just at the time when he wos being dismissed from the AEG as old fashioned. The death of Alfred Messel may, however, have played a part in this.

Behrens both knew and admired most of Walther Rathenau's sculptor friends, all of whom worked in a manner opposed to the taste of the Wilhelmine court (see note 57). He must also have had an equal sympathy for Rathenau's painter friends, who included Max Liebermann and member of the Ber Secession.3 Some Rothenaus literary asociates were also known to Behrens—notably Richard Dehmel, "whose view of the world ploced him next to Rathenau in the eyes of their contemporaries."13s Hugo von Hofmannsthal, Franz Blei, and Julius Meier-Graefe should olso be mentioned. Behrens had first met Hofmannsthal during the preparotions for "Ein Dokument deutscher Kunst," on exhibition held in Darmstodt in 1901.136 Blei and Meier-Graefe both wrote about Behrens from 1897 on. Max Osborn's first article on Behrens appeared one year later, and he also published a piece on Rathenau's Grunewald house in 1912. Several articles devoted to Behrens's work also appeared in Maximilian Harden's magazine Die Zukunft.

It is cleor that Rathenou enjoyed extensive social contacts and friendships with "those literary and artistic circles thot had come to prominence in the bitter feud with the Kaiser over his supreme boycott' 137 and also shared their artistic convictions. However, as suggested obove, his sympathy for modernism wos not unconfined. Rather enigmatically, it did not extend to architecture and the applied arts, the disciplines that most directly concerned the AEG ond those that were practiced by Behrens.

To understand the profound disparity between the views of the man who was to become the president of the AEG and those of the firm's artistic adviser, we must look at the apparently kindred liking for Classicism that these men shared. It cannot be denied that the same Classicism that Rath-tredt eviv faithfully withis house  Grunewald ilustrtion 64) also exercised a dominant influence on Behrens's work at the same time. When Rathenau moved inta his new house in January 1911, Behrens was working an two projects: a villa in Dahlem for Theodor Wiegand, the archealagist and director af the Pergammon Museum,3and the planning stage of the German embassy at St. Petersburg—the major work of this Neo-Classical interlude. '4 The develapment af Behrens's work from the abstract, geometric Classicism af the Düsseldorf and early Berlin period to the more historicist manner of 1911-12 might not have accurred had it not been far the influence of Rathenau. In addition ta any direct influence Rathenau might have had, there was also the version af Neo-Classicism that Messel (whom Behrens admired greatly) had developed far the AEG and its subsidiaries. This interpretation contradicts neither the general tendency toward Neo-Classicism in Germany and especially Berlin during the early years of the twentieth century nor the fundamental differences between the views of architecture held by Rathenau and Behrens. Rathenau combined his desire far an aesthetic revival af early Berlin Classicism with an unmistakable aversion ta the architecture of the Gründerzeit and of his own period. The architect Fritz Schumacher claimed in January 1911, just as Rathenau was moving inta his new house, that he saught, "with biting ridicule, to discredit all the architectural aspirations of the age."41

The house of Walther Rathenau's parents on the Victoriastrasse (illustration 65) also had the character of an aristacratic city residence of araund 1800. It has been atributed to Gabriel von Seidl and to Johannes Kraaz, and dated 1903 or 1910-11. Walther Rathenau collaborated on the graund plan and had rooms in the upper stary.142

In their direct return to the manner of Gilly and Gentz, rather than to that of Schinkel, both Emil and Walther Rathenau saw the realizatian af very personal ideals. Far Emil it symbolized private thrift, absalute realism, and a sober wark ethas. No one knew better than Walther Rathenau that such an image af elegant Prussian austerity was not to be had cheaply, ar mare exactly, that it cost just as much as "cheap" ostentation. According to his definitian,

Elegance means the fantastic expenditure of means and effort in order to create a relatively simple and aesthetically pleasing effect. . . . Luxury and elegance go together just like elegance and austerity. . . . Elegance is controlled extravagance.43

These persanal ideals af the Rathenaus were elevated by Messel in his design far the AEG administration building inta the firm's public image. The "imposing" facade in the style of a palace of the northern Italian Renaissance suggested solidity, pawer, and charisma. In contrast, Messel decarated the office interiors in a manner appraaching the Prussian style of 1800, with a large added shot of Wilhelmine archeology.144 The interior depicted the work ethic for the benefit of the firm's employees, whereas the facade offered the public a symbolic image of well-being and prosperity. In the last years of his life Messel became the principal architect ta the Berlin business world, and worked for Carl Fürstenberg's Berliner Handelsgesellschaft and far Emil Rathenau's AEG. He passed on the principles af his Nea-Classicism with varying success to the younger architects. In the case of Behrens, the impetus of modernity was added to this Berlin Classicism; then the style was catapulted into the mainstream of the European avant-garde by Behrens's students Gropius and Mies. 145

![](../../Industriekultur Peter Behrens and the AEG, 1907-1914 (Buddensieg, Tilmann, Rogge, Henning)/images/18538184491b1d25022a3eafc10d4025acbf93b3bfd852b50d521775b42ad4b8.jpg)

![](../../Industriekultur Peter Behrens and the AEG, 1907-1914 (Buddensieg, Tilmann, Rogge, Henning)/images/4cad3e4e04a3567e17dd42cc3372fc15bd1a08597ce8ef97acf7c2d25d9b06b1.jpg)

In contrast to Messel, however, Behrens was concerned in his study of Prussian Classicism with the contemporary problems of methodology and universal comprehensibility. His aim was the creation of a rotionalized version of Schinkel's Classicism that could be thought of as analogous to the mdusal wor.  Sceffeefi he obl y n1nhesense d' practcal architistincty mo iee u of a conscious, urbone, functional architecture."46 Behrens transformed this Schinkelesque Classicism into a new orchitectural language that could be used with equal facility for administration buildings, such as those of Mannesman in Düsseldorf and Continental in Hanover, or to represent the German stote n the t. Petersburg embassy illustrotion 66. It was olso appropriate for the later factories of the AEG, for exhibition hall, for the l  u a HoetuSanRemt 67, ond for opulent villas such as those for Mertens in Potsdam, Kröller-Müller in the Hague, and Wiegand in Berlin. 149

To Walther Rathenau, Prussian Classicism, and in particulor its polaces and country houses, represented a very personal point of reference. It was the embodiment of his own most intimate set of values, whose modernity lay precisely in its conservatism. It symbolized for him the takeover by the new governing class of bourgeois industriolists. I have already refer o th iea nhealys Hon bus Roeu. I understand Rathenau's emotions on buying the Gily-designed Schloss Freienwolde; the imminent collapse of the house could be seen to stand for "the decline of the aristocracy," which was "without support in the mechanistic society." Similarly, Rathenau's expensive and exoct restoration of the house could be seen as an attempt to legitimize the tokeover. In his biography of Rathenau, Alfred Kerr describes this psychological condition:

Rathenou constontly stressed his frugality (a Diogenes of big industry but in his heod he had an ineradicable passion for country houses. This was not a general passion for the eighteenth century, but simply for the Hohenzollerns in the eighteenth century. Accordingly, when he took over his apartment in the Tiergarten he immediately arronged for the window sills to be token out and the window openings to be extended downwards—it all seemed a bit like Potsdam-Sanssouci. With quiet, holf-suppressed pride, he drew my attention to the alterations. This was long before he bought the former Hohenzollern house, Schloss Freienwalde. I said "I really don't understand it. In almost every respect you're a modern, free-thinking man. You're even, as a true innovator, having the floors in this flat painted gray" (he was the first in Berlin to do this)—"ond yet han San Ro te pas What   paray like in it?"

The Hohenzollern style.

"And what qualities in particular do you admire in the Hohenzollerns?"

"Bu you aren' copyng their simplicity: you'e copying their palaces." He laughed, and I continued: "Do you know what it reminds me of?"

?'

A former once decided to model himself on his neighbor. Strangely enough, the neighbor fed himself only on potatos but drank the finest of wines. The farmer wanted to imitate him exactly and . . . began to drink fine wines."

Rathenau made no angry face. "My dear Doctor," he said, "in these matters one should not only concentrate one's attention on the dynamic aspect, but also on the potential."

I was rather uneasy. He hadn't retaliated and neither could I. So we were even, "fifty-fifty."

Walther Rathenau's very real contemporoneity clothed itself in the "cultil l tablished the boundaries between the world of business and his private life and distinguished among the world of "mechanization," his own social class, and the architectural avant-garde.

Behrens, in contrast, aimed with his modern version of Classicism at a unifying and understandable architectural language. This language could be used not only for the workplaces and homes of the controlling entrepreneurs, but also for those of the managers and workers af a large company like the AEG. It would then serve to define the whole company as "a unified body."3 Behrens's ideal offered a fascinating parallel to the liberal dream of his friend Friedrich Naumann, who advocated the social integration of all the members af the modern industrial society. With his Classicism, Behrens was trying to achieve that social quality Gearg Simmel had ascribed to the art of the Greeks: It was "stylized" and appealed to the entire social body"rather than to the ndividualistic single conmer." Inhisimportant articleaf March 108 n the garden-cty movement, Behrens described the principle of Gruppenbauweise (building in clusters) as a form af living created for "people as social beings," in contrast to the "open" manner of building in the nineteenth century, which isolated the single house with an "exaggerated emphasis on individual and anarchistic independence."155

In the housing estates he built for the AEG before the war, Behrens had the opportunity to implement, if only in part, his far-reaching plans for domesic ousg illustrations A18f.. Stylisticall these estates did notimtate rural models, but turned instead ta the Classicism of the factories. Behrens said, "It would be desirable if, by sharing the same principles, these small estates were able to fuse with the industrial buildings into an organic unity out of which a new and unique sort of industrial city could develop."156

Never had there been a comparable attempt to design a company housing estate using the formal vocabulary of industrial architecture. Fritz Neumeyer has written of the contrast between boulevard architecture and the requirements of a workers' estate, and in this valume he singles out Behre'adaption  ndustrialarchitecture orhousing aniquc rence in the history of damestic architecture. Behrens applied a moderate versian of the unified Classicism of the factaries to the design of dwellings for the AEG's employees, from the workers ta the directors—assuming that the latter were wiling to join in. With the means available to him as an artist, Behrens tried ta realign the inequalities af the social pyramid in the harizantal compositian af pergalas and terraced hauses. It wauld seem that this aim was successful, if one can take the sacial structure of 10-18 Fontanestrasse, Oberschäneweide, as typical: In 1926 the workers Lackner and Janischewski, the electrician Pietrawski, the commercial clerk Schulze, the artist Frisch, the graduate engineer Friedner, and the warks directar Stein all lived on this same street.

As Neumeyer nates below, the "Daric" colonnade (ilustrations A193 ff.), the "entablature," the rectangular towers, and the tympana at Oberschö- neweide support the illusion that the viewer is standing in front af a raw of factory hall." However, Behrens did not use these Classicist farms ta follow the ineteenth-century practicef cladding working-class housng historical forms imitative of the more middle-class apartment blacks and rejecting any modern industrial influence. Rather, he combined the serial possibilities of the Classical farmal language with the industrial demand for standardizatian. The housing that resulted cauld be "built in a ratianal way using standard elements and serial methads af praductian."57 This is a tangible example af Behrens's cancept of industrial Classicism as a new style—"aspra stile nuova"—that would fuse products, producers, housing, and factaries inta "an arganic unity." From its inceptian in 1910, Beens saw his work housigas paf he prace ede every aspect of the AEG's activity. However, only parts of his housing schemes were realized.158

Behrens's AEG housing designs consciously seized on the moteriols ond the forms of the goods praduced by the inhobitants and af the factories in which they worked. The oxiality of the plon for o workers' town thot Behrens published in 1917 in Vom sparsamen Bauen os the result of his plonning work far the AEG connot be included in the conventianal sequence some historions hove drown from the Renoissonce costle to the Baraque palace, the foctories af the nineteenth century, ond the porode grounds af the twentieth-century dictatarships, for Behrens's town wos derived from the vision of o community in which peaple fram all levels of the social pyramid lived ond worked together. Behrens did not drive his oxes thraugh the tawn as a stotement of daminotian, but offered this type of spotiol orgonizotian as an onolagy to Emil Rothenou's production principles. Rotionol ond ecanamic methods of production demonded the oxiolity of the ossembly line, the crane track, ond the overheod tronsporter. In his plon for on industriol town Behrens turned ta pure geametric arder ta creote o visuol onology thot could embroce the foctaries, the smollest opplionces, ond the housing. Wolther Rothenou's ideos on the quosigovernmentol structure af lorge industrial orgonizotions found here a caunterport in the residents' layalty ta the firm. The sense af group identificotion promated by Behrens's design wos quite different, however, from the closs-specific salidarity thot found expression in the suburbs built in Berlin in the 1920s by Bruno Tout ond Mortin Wogner. One was the response af an enlightened employer to the challenge of sociol democrocy; the other wos o model plon of sacial democrocy itself, which could be delivered repeatedly by the same orchitects.

It is clear that Behrens also took the specific modernity of his Classicism as the basis for his work in product design. We have already seen that, in designing technical appliances, Behrens was not content to ignore the electrical components, but looked instead for  form in which the compoents and their functions could express themselves. Here to he assumed a position which it would be inexact to call neutral. The difficulty and delicacy of this position stemmed from Behrens's rejection of the twin temptations of nineteenth-century product design as defined by Karl Scheffler—Behrens resolved neither to "decorate" the applionces nor to abandon them to "the prosaic hideousness, the absolute formlessness, of the naked production process." The resulting forms appropriate to the type, the series, and the standard model could get along quite happily without the help of historical retrospection or eclecticism: "Although one senses the link back to the Classical codex, one is still very aware of the steely present. One expects to find and looks for the machines."

With his epoch-making designs for the turbine factory and the arc lomps, Behrens created—effortlessly, it seemed—self-explanatory analogies linking industry, art, and Classicism. After this dazzling beginning, however, he turned increasingly to history to provide the support and the justification he needed in his difficult task. After 1913 the precarious balance between the "Classical codex" and the "present"' tended to favor the consolidation of troditional values. With the factories in Oberschöneweide, the Berlin exhibition halls, 160 the Festhalle at the 1914 Werkbund exhibition, 16 the Volkshaus in Lübeck, 162 and the AEG administration at Humboldthain (illustration A163), Behrens sought to secure and promote everything of universol and binding value that he had ochieved in his work for the AEG. This dream of universality was another parallel to Wolther Rathenau's thoughts on the quasi-governmentol structure of major firms like thEG.Word War Ihathis bel inhe possibility e style representative of the nation's industrial power as an alternative to the international monarchic style. The fragments Behrens produced at the end of his life now appear Expressionistic, culminating in the mysticism of the Memorial Hall at Höchst. The steely present," which had been cepted so emphaticaly and endowed with the legitimacy of Classicism, had led into the catastrophe. The analogies of industriol production were replaced in the short term by the Gothic hereafter and the hondcrofted past.

According to Osthaus, Behrens's prime concern was to bring art back into the production process Thaggravated the isagreement between Behrens and Walther Rathenau, and extended it into another area of mutual interest: fine art. The attempt to forge on olliance between art and technology, between technical norms and oesthetic types, between industrial mass production ond the universal disseminotion of the collective style of a future industrial culture—in Rathenau's eyes all this represented the Fall, the moral ruin that "dragged art down into the dust and noise of everyday life."

Although he granted continued existence to all the other arts, Rathenou denied the possibility thot architecture and the applied arts could recover from the ravages of the Gründerzeit. Not only did he deplore the fact that architecture had sunk "to the level of o supplier of art-historical decoration," but he saw the future of orchitecture as "rretrievably destroyed—in the sense of genuine creative design—by the mechanization of the production process." "Architecture", said Rathenau, "concluded the list of major victims" of mechonization. Furthermore, the "dreodful decline of the applied arts over the past eighty years, which even the most earnest endeavors have failed to halt," was a legacy, he said, of mechanization.

To Walther Rathenau, the reason far the demise of architecture and craftsmanship as true art forms lay in their servitude to functianalism, t "commercial mativation," and ta the functianal demands made by industry. For this reason he drew a sharp distinction between architecture and the applied arts, on ane hand, and literature, music, and the plastic arts on the ather: "Art daes nat have ta fulfil any purpose whatsaever, be it decorative, representative, instructive, entertaining, morally edifying, recollective, or stimulating to the senses." Among the functions that corrupted art, Rathenau included not only such "ignoble" concerns as "advertising and business," but also "instruction, entertainment, . .. decaratian, and sensary stimulatian." The cancept af art was thus restricted ta an extreme subjectivity, which Rathenau uncompromisingly pursued. He demonstrated the private nature of his own version af Classicism with his rejection of the Clasical language Behrens had developed for the initilly unsympathetic executives of the AEG. Rathenau designed his Grunewald house as a retrospective gesture, using forms taken from preindustrial architecture. Yet bath Rathenau and Behrens invaked the same system of values to justify their radically different versians of Classicism. Rathenau turned to Classicism in order to make a private statement about the absalute incampatability of business, art, and individuality; Behrens used it ta farmulate a restarative synthesis af the three.

Although Walther Rathenau felt that technical inventians cauld "at no time be complete and perfect," he admitted that they cauld be "beautiful." However, he had reservatians about the quality of this beauty which, he said, "is perceived by the engineer and affected and overrated by the aesthete":

. . . half af this beauty depends on rational understanding and is therefore ephemeral. If a machine is totally obsalete, then it might still retain a picturesque quality, . . . but the unprejudiced eye will see it as junk, as a dunct nk nscientiically interestig develapment  [without last worth.

It was, according to Rathenau, "an outrageous abuse" to provide everyday appliances with a form that gave them the appearance of works of art.

On 27 November 1905, Rathenau wrote to Felix Holländer, the dramatic adviser to Max Reinhardt: "Much as I venerate art and respect commerce, I find the border areas between the two odious and suspect."164 This maxim, which Rathenau was ta repeat on ather occasions, struck at the heart of Behrens's design philosophy and at the grounds for his appointment by the AEG. We are confronted here by a paradox: Rathenau could envisage a conflict-free, socially mobile future society, such as Behrens was attempting ta create far the AEG, yet he had nothing but criticism for Behrens's visual reorganizatian, which was the first stage in this process. Clearly, Rathenau's critical comments were directed not only at the culture of the Gründerzeit but also at Behrens's work for the AEGthe most comprehensive attempt made this century ta win back a place for art in the practice of industrial productian, and with it a social dimension. It followed from Behrens's ideas that art should not be constrained by Rathenau's paradoxical definitian; that it should not remain "free of purpase and function" merely to suit the private purposes af thase who could affard it.

Detailed study af Rathenau's writings and speeches (facilitated greatly by Ernst Schulin's edition) shows that, although Rathenau was very interested in Behrens's work for the AEG and recognized it as an "earnest endeavor," he aften commented on comparable reforming experiments in terms that vacillated between skepticism and irony, between sympathy and antipathy.

Already in 1912, as Behrens's newly refurbished AEG stood before him, Rae ed e elestpt me he imposed by mechanistic function":

Man has the feeling that he once possessed some things that were irreplaceable; he is now artfully trying to win back what has been lost by planting little sanctuaries in his mechanized world, such as roof gardens on top of factories. Here is a nature cult dragged out of the past, therea superstition, the dream of a communal existence, a sham naivety, a false sense of jollity, a purified Christianity, an archaism, a stylization. . Nevertheless, this game does not deserve our contempt, for it arises out of a passionate longing. Yet it remains helpless and childish, for arcadian groves do not flourish on the shaking ground of mechanization. 165

Here Rathenau was referring explicily to the roof garden atop the old machine factory on the Brunnenstrasse (illustration 69) as an example of the hopelessness of all efforts to elevate aesthetics into an exclusive first principle. Behrens had redesigned and replanted this roof garden in 1910 for the guests of the AEG.

Similarly, Rathenau said that the "strident, hastily conceived" forms of technical appliances had little chance of competing with hand-produced objects developed over the centuries. With his predilection for eschatological dreams of a future which could not be rushed or hurried into existence and his belief in the continuity of the past, Rathenau repeatedly criticized "hurriedly thought-out design,""novelty,""reckless imitation," and "ephemerality."

Architecture, this "most circumspect of all the arts" was finished, said Rathenau—"irredeemably destroyed by the mechanization of production." All attempts to invent new building regulations, styles of ornament, and art forms with pen and ink" were "doomed to pitiful failure." He deplored the fact that the lonic capital and entablature were no longer used in architectural design and had been superseded by "a riveted construction that would be aesthetically pleasing and technically clever in the desin of a lamp." It is very likely that Rathenau was referrig directy here to the riveted construction Bernhard and Behrens had used in the turbine hall.

![](../../Industriekultur Peter Behrens and the AEG, 1907-1914 (Buddensieg, Tilmann, Rogge, Henning)/images/2002e103dd4319c00946ccc122e5c1c3c9cbc9d88b26e373306b16017d66d35f.jpg)  
69 Peter Behrens, AEG roof gorden, Brunnenstrosse. AEG photo 10514.

Rathenau uncompramisingly defended art's absolute freedom from function and its subjective nature. Any attempt to enlist the help af art ta alleviate the effects of mechanization was bound to fail, for art was an unsuitable means for this purpose. Art abused in this way, said Rathenau, "extols the distorted and the false by means of sentimentality and mechanical path"trives oachieve the rongest possible contrast with the previus cansumer practice" (thereby "treading the path of fashion"), and "carefully assembles trash and baubles far glassy display and expensive sale." This could be seen as Rathenau's commentary on Behrens's redesigning of the AEG's arc lamps, kettles, factories, sales literature, and retail shaps. One cauld almast think that Rathenau was making a direct reference to Arthur Fürst's Berliner Tageblatt article (repraduced below), which lauded in Behrens's work all the attributes Rathenau scarned. To Rathenau, such things as "daring design," "reckless imitation," the sudden arrival of "hastily canceived forms," and much-publicized breaks with earlier versions of an appliance were all "whipped up by the competition at the tianshedes eplait anything that was succesu et Rathenau, was merely an attempt to improve on the banal, the "propping up" af that which was "distorted and false." He reacted no less angrily to those people who "weave the mean implements of modern life into their sensibilities" and "write af arc lamps and hotel gardens in rhymes of deliberate daring."167 In 1916, one year befare the publicatian af Rathenau's Von kommenden Dingen, the Inselbücherei braught out Theodor Däubler's Das Sternenkind. Among this collection of poems was ane entitled "Diadem":

The arc lamps crown the sunset,   
Their laays will tve the vg,   
Spectrally they flaat above the noisy throng   
Like vitrified fruits from ather worlds.   
I find it hard ta comprehend the essence of these lamps. The stars seems wise, the moon will gladly be angry, Why do you turn pale under the starry diadem?

According to Rathenau, a poet wha wrote on such themes was not a prophet,"but merely  supplier of aesthetic diversian.

Rathenau also categarically opposed the "optimists" who "hope that every new invention, form of transportatian, or occupation will produce new artistic forms." To "sing the praises of mechanization"was, he said, like "discussing with a pit pony the strangeness of its situation."68 He cannot, therefore, have had much sympathy for the drawings and watercolors of the AEG factories done by Lovis Corinth and Hans Baluschek (illustrations 7072),69 and Walter Dexel's 1917 painting "Arc Lamps" (illustration 3) must have eceived the same daning criticimas Däuler's poem.

Who did Rathenau mean when he spoke of the "insufferable boasters who extol every mechanized necessity to us as a step toward perfectian"? He never tired of comparing the "ald things from the age of craftsmanship with the mass junk of our conser industry."Even thaug it s passible that he was referring here to the cheap goods sold in the department stores, his accusation that "the calculated rhetaric af machine ornament" was used ta "mack the old appliances" was also applicable to Behrens's ceiling fans, to the surface decoration of his kettles, and to his electric heaters.

We cannot take a step without being accompanied and surrounded by arty drawings. Adornments of calor and form are applied to every utilitarian object; the typefaces that talk to us during the day have artistic pretensions. . . . The whale warld is sweating art from every pore." This could also be an allusian ta the graphic-design work Behrens did for the AEG's brochures and pamphlets. Against the "tarturaus" efforts of artists like Behrens, Rathenau set "true art," which made tangible "the laws af the organic world, af destiny, of the soul, and of the divine." This could best be achieved, said Rathenau, by music, "the true art af aur time." By making spiritual experience accessible to us, music "is transfigured into a pure work af the spirit." The music room in his house in Darmstadt suggests that Behrens would probably have agreed with this sentiment, perhaps with the reservatian that one shauld not see transfiguration as the only function of art.

![](../../Industriekultur Peter Behrens and the AEG, 1907-1914 (Buddensieg, Tilmann, Rogge, Henning)/images/5425bf6273bbca175a9b893bffb6d97ec4a0f321f58c89f33741ea049d2648c7.jpg)

![](../../Industriekultur Peter Behrens and the AEG, 1907-1914 (Buddensieg, Tilmann, Rogge, Henning)/images/6f7f0bf4a7fa1e1f85df091ac215d99cc696f9f9dcf91bb3cae67a4af99c00a2.jpg)
